B002RVZV9K|5.0|\\"It Might Get Loud\\" is awesome.  I wish they'd turn this into a series, with a bunch of different artists.  The concept of pulling guitarists from several different bands into a room together to discuss their equipment, techniques, styles, strings... it's just an awesome little voyeuristic adventure that fans eat up, and fellow musicians understand.  I can't recommend this highly enough.  As a musician, it's totally understood.  I played in a rehearsal studio for several years where a dozen or more bands were in and out all the time.  When we finished our practice, I would wander down the hall and listen to what other bands were playing.  If I liked it, I'd knock on the door, and if allowed in, I'd hang out and talk 'shop' with the other musicians.  Everyone always comes out enriched from such experiences.  It is a stroke of genius that someone orchestrated this and filmed it.  Carry on, carry on!  We need more of this!  Make a sequel!  Make several!

0307454541|3.0|There are plenty of reviews already showing you what the book is about. Instead, as someone who lived in Scandinavia for a few years, I will try to tell you my opinion about what this book means, where it comes from and why the hype about it.<br /><br />While many readers from the US commented on the weird characters, this book accurately depicts the world as seen through the eyes of a significant share of Scandinavians. Remember, the original title is \\"Men that hate women\\". In detail, that world-view is the following:<br /><br />1) The only differences between men and women are the negative male traits<br />2) A superior/civilized man is one that has no masculine traits whatsoever and accepts the superiority of women<br />3) Despite the female superiority, due to the remaining uncivilized evil men and the still patriarchic society, women are victims of men<br />4) Right-wing white men are the epitome of evilness<br />5) Left-wing feminist women are the epitome of goodness<br /><br />To understand the power of this book and why it became a best-seller, you have to understand its cathartic role:<br />In Sweden, women are told every day how much they suffer in the hands of men and men are told every day how evil they are. That breeds resentment and guilt.<br /><br />Suddenly, unlike the books you get to read from foreign authors (Dan Brown does not get even close to Stieg Larsson), a book comes along that depicts the world as you were told it the world is like: The rich are evil; women are all sexual victims of men; women are all dominant, wise, energetic, confident and strong; cherry on the cake, women finally have the justification to express their anger through physical violence towards men.<br /><br />Women in Scandinavia read through the pages of this book totally absorbed and finish the book feeling avenged and understood.<br /><br />This book became a hit neither because it is a good thriller nor because it was better in the Swedish version. No; it became a hit because it is a manifesto for the version of feminism that is mainstream in Scandinavia.<br />For readers unaware of the ideology, the characters will seem awkward and unbelievable.<br />For readers grown into that ideology, the book is just logical.<br />For readers wanting to learn the ideology and the Swedish culture of today, the book is an open window.<br /><br />Despite its major shortcomings as a novel, I nevertheless recommend reading this book if you are interested in seeing the world through leftist-feminist glasses. If you are not into that, you better skip it altogether.<br /><br />Rod Govea, author of [[ASIN:8564329026 Here She Comes! The Experienced Man's Sex Guide to Deeply Understanding His Woman, Driving Her Crazy with Desire, Giving Her Ever Stronger Orgasms]]tream in Scandinavia.  For readers unaware of the ideology, the characters will seem awkward and unbelievable.  For readers grown into that ideology, the book is just logical.  For readers wanting to learn the ideology and the Swedish culture of today, the book is an open window.     Despite its major shortcomings as a novel, I nevertheless recommend reading this book if you are interested in seeing the world through leftist-feminist glasses. If you are not into that, you better skip it altogether.    Rod Govea, author of [[ASIN:8564329026 Here She Comes! The Experienced Man's Sex Guide to Deeply Understanding His Woman, Driving Her Crazy with Desire, Giving Her Ever Stronger Orgasms]]

B003Y5H542|5.0|4.8 stars<br /><br />Rango is no kids' flick, but it is a mature masterpiece in a whole bunch of ways that will soon make it a major cult favorite, guaranteed. It takes a certain sense of humor to dig this one, but if you've got it, you're in for a big big treat. Rango also has one of the best-looking, most detailed blu-ray transfers I've yet seen.<br /><br />A partial remake of Chinatown with existential overtones and psychedelic undercurrent with nods to dozens of cinematic classics, this \\"cartoon\\" Western satire has a lot more in common with Bugs Bunny and his anarchic freakiness than with any of the new crop of Pixar etc family animation works, great as they can be (Up, Incredibles). It also has Roger Rabbit's tantalizing self-reflexivity in spades and glittering diamonds. Rango salutes HST in its first scene and then goes waaaay off into left field, climbs into the bleachers, gets a beer, watches the relievers warm up, and finally scampers back down into right field and is kicked out of the ball park.<br /><br />For me it was a major treat, as I love Thompson, Chinatown, Warner Bros cartoons, and intelligent insanity in general. Rango piles on so many genres and ideas and spoofed cliches and wowza that your head is spinning most of the time, even if just from the truly amazing animation quality and endless wild concepts. Logan's script is so much brighter and sharper than almost anything being made lately that it seems an anachronism; its levels of thought will take your brain to many literate, self-aware clouds floating by your mind's eye.<br /><br />Verbinski directs the proceedings with similar aplomb, and Depp shows more voice and character range than in almost anything he's ever done. Ned Beatty is another standout, but everyone is excellent. And the various little conspiracy nods are all too accurate; there's a lot going on in Rango, and multiple viewings will show many layers of crisp detail, visually and intellectually. One might even call this a kaleidoscopic environmentalist's lament, a world-weary nod to how close to the brink we really are and a reminder that there are many other worlds out there and in here, so let's watch it Buddha-like with as much compassionate detachment as is musterable. Whatevar that means!<br /><br />And eye candy? Man, this is the handmade organic 75% cacao chocogasm of eye candy. Soooo much detail in some of the animals' hair and eyes that it sets a new standard (can't wait to see it in 3D). This will show off your flat screen in ways you'd never imagined. It's so good I unplugged my PS3 and took it to a friend's place just so he could see how good his flat screen can look. I wouldn't go to that trouble for just any film.<br /><br />Nor would I watch it twice in two days. I haven't done that in years, in fact. But I just had to check to see if Rango was as beguilingly weird and wonderful by daylight as it was late at night, and indeed it is.<br /><br />Rango is bound to be hailed as one of the great toon films of all time, methinks, and I'd rank it with Roger Rabbit, the best Bugs (high praise, bub!), Up and a very few other animated masterpieces. Is it for kids? Not really, unless you'd like to raise them to be smarter and more self-aware than the average little texting robots these days, so, you know, be careful! I think many kids are much brighter much earlier than we think, but then again I watched a lot of Bugs as a kid, so I may be biased.<br /><br />If you've got a blu-ray player, at least rent this just to see how good your screen can look. And if you happen to get your mind blown in the process, well, lucky you! It beats being a thirsty lizard in an aloha shirt.nt, a world-weary nod to how close to the brink we really are and a reminder that there are many other worlds out there and in here, so let's watch it Buddha-like with as much compassionate detachment as is musterable. B004IQMXD4|4.0|Upon my first listen of IN YOUR DREAMS, Stevie Nicks' first new studio effort in a decade, I was really concerned...the disc just left me cold. Nothing jumped out at me. Even the two albums that I've long considered her weakest ~ 1985's ROCK A LITTLE and STREET ANGEL from 1994 ~ each had three or four cuts that I instantly loved. With so many people saying this was a \\"masterpiece\\" and a \\"career-defining\\" release, could I be missing something?!?<br /> Then I remembered....more and more, it seems like you just can't judge an album by it's album cover...in other words, give it a few spins! Case in point...I hated the new Bob Schneider after my first listen, while I loved the new one from The Cars. Now, the Bob Schneider is one of my favorite albums of the year, while the Cars...not so much!<br /> But back to Stevie. After a few more listens, it hit me...this isn't the same Bella Donna/Gypsy/Rhiannon that we've come to know, love and respect over the last 35+ years. No, this is a more grounded, mature 63 year old woman who's seeing life through a vastly different crystal ball (Hey, she hasn't TOTALLY changed!!). And I like it!<br /> Oh, I still have some issues, mainly with the production...I think Dave Stewart and (especially!) Glen Ballard are SO wrong for Nicks (I hope this is a one time teaming, and GOD FORBID Stewart helms the next Fleetwood Mac effort!)....she needs someone a little more...dangerous, with a little more edge and freshness. Someone like Rick Rubin, or Matt Serlatic. (Or, based on the one cut that he co-produced here, Mike Campbell). That said, though, I'm happy to say that, overall, I am now loving this disc.<br /> The album opens with it's first single, the relaxed, slinky mid-tempo \\"Secret Love.\\" The arrangement lopes along at a nice pace , while Nicks' voice has aged like a fine wine, with her lower register in particular coming across more powerful than ever. Mark my word, twenty-five years ago, this would have been a HUGE hit for Nicks.<br /> \\"For What It's Worth\\" is one of the disc's major surprises for me. Initially dismissing the track, I now absolutely love it. Heartfelt, deeply sincere lyrics and a gorgeous, country-tinged arrangement all come together to brilliant effect. However, the true star here is actually Nicks' vocal(s)....her lead is lovely, and when she starts to harmonize with herself on backing vocals....WOW!! Simply amazing! In a just world this track, which was just chosen as the second single, will be an across the board hit for her.<br /> On the flip side, the album's frantic title track does nothing for me. It's almost as if, knowing what a huge hit Tom Petty's \\"I Need To Know\\" has been for Nicks in concert, Stewart tried to recapture that classic's energy and drive. Not even close, Dave...not even close!<br /> \\"Wide Sargasso Sea\\" also fails to ignite...it's more like a tabloid article set to music...who's she singing about (Rupert Hine)? Were they really that in love (Guess so)? And DID he ever forget \\"that kiss\\" (We'll never know!)? Nicks' vocal is somewhat flat, but I do like the almost tribal vibe that the track wraps up on. Not a total disaster, but a miss none the less.<br /> Far stronger is the evocative \\"New Orleans.\\" The Crescent City really is becoming quite the star...Steve Earle also sings about her on his excellent new I'LL NEVER GET OUT OF THIS WORLD ALIVE. Here, Nicks' atmospheric, thoughtful lyrics dance over a beautiful arrangement, all muted instrumentation and full-blown feeling. Add a lead vocal full of wonder and amazement and the aforementioned insightful lyrics (\\"Where the good and the righteous walk/And the wicked as well\\"), and you have a lovely little gem. (And those closing chords...!).<br /> \\"Moonlight (A Vampire's Dream)\\" is another one of the tracks that just doesn't...gel. Nicks has been here before, and to much better effect (\\"Beauty & The Beast\\"). O.K......but not great.<br /> A lot of people have been less than thrilled about \\"Annabel Lee\\", but I digit. What's not to love?!? The arrangement chugs along beautifully, the lead vocal soars, and how can you go wrong with lyrics adapted from the works of Edgar Allen Poe??? This is everything \\"Moonlight\\" isn't. Love it!<br /> Knowing how much \\"Soldier's Angel\\" means to Nicks, I REALLY hate what I'm about to say, but...this song's a disaster. Yes, the lyrics are honest and real, but everything else is a mess, from the vocals (Nicks' are nasally and robotic, while Lindsey Buckingham's are sharp and shrill) to the arrangement (cold and dissonant). A song this tender and raw needs the acoustic warmth of, say, \\"Has Anyone Ever Written Anything For You\\" or \\"I Sing For The Things\\". Instead, we get a stark, militaristic...roll call. A major, MAJOR misstep.<br /> \\"Everybody Loves You\\" is a nice mid-tempo album cut, featuring a crisp duet between Nicks and Stewart. The lyrics are a little heavy handed ~ almost a case of trying too hard ~ but, overall, this is an O.K. cut.<br /> And then there's \\"Ghosts Are Gone.\\" Without a doubt my favorite track on IN YOUR DREAMS, I love this cut not because it's probably the closest thing there is to a classic Stevie Nicks rocker, but because of it's attitude and edge, it's overall SASS! Toss in lyrics that conjure up visions of a sequel to \\"Angel\\" (probably my favorite Nicks song of all time!), and you have an absolute winner!<br /> At this point in time, I never thought I would ever be shocked by Stevie Nicks again...I was pretty sure she had played all her cards. Well, \\"shocked\\" is the only way I can describe my reaction to \\"You May Be The One.\\" All bluesy and torchy, this song is a vocal triumph...I have NEVER heard Nicks sound like this! Between this beaut and her cover of Etta James' \\"At Last\\" a decade or so ago, I would LOVE to see that album of standards that Nicks has been talking about for years come to fruition. The disc's true masterpiece (Plus, the lyrics are really fun..which member of Fleetwood Mac is she talking about in which verse?!?)!<br /> The only word that be used to describe \\"Italian Summer\\" is \\"epic\\" ~ this track is like a mini movie musical. All strings and passion and stormy weather, the cut is just gorgeous, anchored by a lead vocal that swoops and shimmers. Breathtakingly beautiful, this stunner should have been the disc's power house closer.<br /> Instead, that task unfortunately falls upon the limp \\"Cheaper Than Free.\\" Weak vocals, weak lyrics, weak everything, the track's all around inferiority is just made more evident following on the heels of the vastly superior \\"Italian Summer.\\"<br /> That said, what are my overall thoughts on IN YOUR DREAMS? Well, while I don't think it's one of Stevie Nicks' BEST albums to date, I do think it's one of her most IMPORTANT. I think she's crossed over to a land where gypsies and Welch witches still dance, but where she can also now do ANYTHING....that aforementioned standards album...or that country one....or that Christmas one...or that one of duets...or that one of covers with songs all written by guys!! Whatever the choice may be, I don't think that, from this point on, Nicks will be contemplating it solely in her dreams. (As with all my reviews, I'm giving the disc an extra half a star for including the lyrics).ent chugs along beautifully, the lead vocal soars, and how can you go wrong with lyrics adapted from the works of Edgar Allen Poe??? 0553801473|5.0|A Dance with Dragons is probably the least 'epic' of the books so far; it is far more inclined to be intimate with characters and their relationships. And I loved it.<br /><br />George is a top notch character writer. Every one of his characters is multifaceted and realistic; having goals, wants, needs, and lusts that befit a real human being. B003EYVY04|1.0|If you're like me and are ignoring the reviews for this product that complain about it not containing the extended version because that doesn't bother you, please read the following. The main reason I wanted this DVD was to add to my growing collection of HP Ultimate Editions to make for a very nice, orderly, congruent display on my shelf. The first two movies were released quite nicely; the book-like DVD case was wrapped in a vertical slip cover. Simple, effective. Here's how this release screwed up:    - Lenticular cover. Looks nice I guess, but it's ultimately pointless and it makes the first two releases (which don't have one) stand out. Also, it's extremely difficult to remove the lenticular picture. I admit that you'd probably wouldn't want to (there's nothing behind it! Not even a normal cover), but it's not glued in place so it almost looks like they thought you might want to. How is it held in? Read on.    - Lenticular cover is held in by corner flaps that, if you're not careful, will probably be caught on other DVDs as you slide them onto the shelf next to it. Corner flaps are the \\"3\\" and dragon seen on the cover as well as the \\"Ultimate Edition\\" title on the adjacent corner. There is NO way this can stay mint unless you're EXTREMELY careful.    - Horizontal slip cover. Technically a flimsy cardboard slip cover isn't ideal in any case, but if it's vertical it's a bit easier to be careful. Horizontal (which is, admittedly, how most box sets are done-- for example, if you have the 2004 Original Trilogy Star Wars DVDs box set, it's the same thing there), because of the way it needs to be folded to construct the box, leaves a tab of cardboard on the inside, pointing toward where you insert the DVD. If you're not careful it can catch, and start degrading your already very flimsy set up that you're trying to keep nice looking. I had to tape mine down which I was only okay with because it's in the inside. But I really shouldn't have to.    - Security tab stuck HALFWAY UNDER one end of the aforementioned cardboard tab, jutting out one end of it making it almost guaranteed to catch the DVD and get bent. I know Amazon probably orders these from the same place any other store does, but really? Security tags? It wasn't impossible to remove but it did take some work as it was stuck on pretty good. Real pain in butt.    - This box set is TALLER than the previous two! Not by much, it's certainly noticeable. It's possible it's because of the horizontal slip cover design (at least two bits of cardboard on the top and bottom that aren't there on the previous two releases), but it's still inexcusable. Part of the reason collectors like sets like these is because it makes movie collections look like one complete saga rather than a mindless cash grab. How much each of these sets look like part of one complete series will make or break them in collectors' eyes.    - The flap on the book-like DVD cover is no longer magnetic like the previous two releases. Not that big of a deal, but it was kind of nice to have that.     If you are like me, you'll probably want this set anyway because just having the first two years just doesn't cut it (and if you're REALLY like me, year 3 is your favorite HP movie so it'll be even harder to avoid). Just know what you're getting into. Movie studios don't have much of an incentive to make a set that will last a long time and be completely perfect because then they won't have a reason to sell you more copies of it later. Hopefully, if there's a box set that includes every movie in the future, it will be better than this and hopefully include all of the one hour documentaries that each of these releases have (another reason I'm buying these). But for now, this isn't the \\"Ultimate Edition\\" by any definition.    One final note: This set and the Year 4 set was released last year (2010), but I bought it recently. If I recall correctly, when I saw Year 3 and Year 4 in stores they didn't have that lenticular cover,but I notice the Year 5 and Year 6 DVDs do. This might be a new thing they're doing for 2011, and if they are still making the previous releases, they might have retroactively added those covers. If so, it might be worth your time to carefully research which version you're buying (and perhaps go back and get newer versions of Year 1 and Year 2 if they exist). That is if you value a matching set more than decently constructed DVD cases, though. Just a thought.

When you look up the definition of portable it states, \\"able to be easily carried or moved,\\" or as a noun, \\"a version of something, such as a small lightweight television or computer, that can be easily carried.\\" Ok now that I got that out of the way on to the review.<br /><br />The screen delivers a great picture and yes the viewing angles to the sides are not the best but it was only designed with one to two players in mind. You can adjust the EQ settings and get a little more punch from them but don't expect to get amazing sound. I prefer to use headsets anyway because I do not like to disturb others and you pick up sound cues better then TV speakers. There are two headphone jacks right below the screen which is nice when the parents want to keep the noise down but the kids want to get their game on or vice versa. :D<br /><br />The case itself is very well built. I do not fear dropping the case because it is padded and designed to be safe for transportation. The handle has a nice grip to it and I believe it holds the case very well. The latches on the G155 are sturdy and it is nice that they were designed to include the option of placing a lock on either side of the case. I do not find it hard to carry around despite its size. It has two mesh-like bags inside that hold your cords and controllers. I have been able to fit all my cords and a controller inside them. For those who look at the straps that secure the xbox 360 or PS3 to the case, it does not block the vent and does not overheat the console. I had my xbox 360 going for about four hours straight and while it did get warm, it did not overheat or seem too hot. My home xbox 360 gets about the same temperature and it is out in the open with plenty of air and room.<br /><br />Overall, Gaems designed a safe, secure and fun product that casual to hardcore gamers can use when they are at home or not. I would have preferred to have the remote included, a shoulder strap, better speakers, a way to carry a game or two inside the case and maybe a way to adjust the level of the screen once the case is opened. However, for the price, it is very rewarding. I travel a lot and there is down time from time to time at my work and I bring it in to play during those times and at lunch. I have not been disappointed by this purchase.  The G155 is worth the investment and I highly recommend it.<br /><br />On a side but equally important note, Customer Service from the Gaems crew is outstanding. They have a specifically made Twitter account just to get support and they are always willing to help and take in new ideas. Third, I am also huge fan of prog rock, especially Jethro Tull and Yes. Last of all, I think progressive rock/metal is not confined to odd signatures, sign changes, virtuosity, etc. (which I will call \\"straightforward prog\\" in this review), but do something novel in terms of music. The reason, I write this list at the beginning of my review is to stress that you're reading a biased review (just like all other reviews), but at least you can decide on whether to buy the album or not, based on whether my tastes are similar to yours.    A few misconceptions of the album. This is NOT Paradise Lost II! PL was more of a straightforward prog metal album. This album is purely heavy metal. Actually, this how Black Sabbath should have sounded if they had progressed from '75. Here comes to the second misconception: \\"this album is not progressive metal, we miss old SyX\\". Again wrong! If you listen closely, you can hear almost everything about old SyX. The great achievement of this album (and Michael Romeo) is that this album succeeds in combining everything cool about metal music in the same pot. You hear a thrash metal riff, backed up with contemporary classical influenced choral and keyboard work, and this has never been more fluid even for  SyX previously. Or you can hear some very fast interludes of guitar and drum, but not exactly you would hear from a thrash band, but with subtle proggy touches. It is easily possible to miss these \\"progressive\\" parts, but that is the main success of the album. These are metal songs, but written in a novel way. The questions that I ask to decide whether an album is great or not are, whether the songs are great, and whether this album will be musically influential. Both answers are YES! I think that Iconoclast will create a new metal style, combiningheavy riffs, melodic stuff, with straightforward prog metal.    Those who think V is better, please realize V was based on Verdi's theme with John Williams styles themes repeated through the whole album, and with some Mozart also. Though V was a great album, what we get at the end is a very nice adaptation of these original masters to heavy metal.. Now we have 100% pure SyX, and they are getting even better with each release.

Why am I saying this? I think Martin's writing is much more mature in this book than in the previous ones. We get a much more intimate view into the minds of the different point of view characters.    (The following explanations may contain spoilers for some, beware.)    I loved the way we could glimpse into the minds of Cersei and Jaime. Their chapters are simply brilliantly written. Just a little example to show Martin's new way of letting us go deeper into the characters: Whenever Jamie looks at Cersei flirting or just being nice with other men he always remembers Tyrion's remark \\"...she's been ****ing Lancel and Osmund Kettleback and Moonboy for all I know...\\" This little sentence appears at least a hundred times in Jamie's chapters, and it does not become boring, on the contrary. It shows how tormented and jealous Jamie is. Repetition is a powerful way to emphasize emotions; Martin uses it to a great effect.    The previous example was only one out of many. I enjoyed Cersei's chapters, too. It is a bold an welcome change that a really \\"evil\\" character was chosen as an important point of view character for this book. It is good that we get to know what motivates this almost insane woman, and how power and unjustified self-confidence can lead to a borderline personality.    I can understand that after the furious and action-packed \\"Storm of Swords\\" some readers felt that this book is a disappointment. Not for me. But those who need battles and sword-fights and characters dying each chapter may feel cheated. Please, look more closely. This book is a gem. You can rarely read a better description of what psychological effects war and power have on people's minds.     All in all, definitely a five-star book, but I must admit this is not everybody's piece of cake.

Led by the fiery declaration \\"I'm Dat Chick,\\"  in which Rowland thrusts \\"I tell them keep on talking cause` I love the attention,\\" \\"Here I Am\\" packs enough punch to excite listeners for some time but will likely be drowned out by more pressing and inventive tunes from others. Perhaps it is the buzz behind \\"Motivation\\" that raised expectations, add to that a defining performance at the 2011 BET Awards, but \\"Here I Am\\" leaves you wavering between commendation and boredom after a few listens. That is not to say \\"Here I Am\\" is an effort filled with mediocrity, it is, like a lot of albums today, one that has its timeless songs and others that last for a fleeting moment.    Three tracks that stand out, beyond the already successful \\"Motivation\\" and \\"Commander,\\" are \\"Turn It Up,\\" \\"Feelin Me Right Now,\\" and \\"All of the Night\\".    \\"Turn It Up,\\" an infectious record that kicks off with Rowland singing \\"I could tear the whole world down,\\" fits well with her vocal prowess. Apt, \\"Turn It Up\\" follows the similarly fluid \\"Feeling Me Right Now\\". Daring, like \\"Motivation\\" and \\"I'm Dat Chick,\\" this song finds Rowland singing \\"don't you leave without my handcuffs, I made some special plans for us tonight\\". If the sex-driven songs are not your thing, Kelly does take a moment to recognize relationships, at least her man in particular. Drafting fellow Houston-native Lil Playy, Rowland attempts to applaud the love of a \\"Work It Man\\" who \\"ain't scared\\" to carry her baggage. That all goes south however, on \\"Heaven & Earth,\\" a strong ballad (one of few on the album, \\"Keep It Between Us\\" another),  as she cries out \\"no more feeling sorry for myself cause I know my worth... because I know I deserve heaven and earth,\\" as she kicks her man to the curb.    While Kelly spends much of this album playing with radio friendly R&B/Urban Pop, sans \\"Lay It On Me,\\" she revisits the music that earned her much solo success in recent years -- dance. Led by the chart-topping single \\"Commander,\\" Rowland picks up where she left off with \\"When Love Takes Over\\" on songs like the stomp worthy \\"Down For Whatever\\" followed by the bonus track \\"Each Other,\\" an equally infectious record perfect for clubs.    Overall, Kelly Rowland deserves praise for taking a chance with this album, arguably her best effort to date. While it lacks the punch necessary to make it a smash, it is a worthy upgrade that could lead to greater solo success. After all, this is \\"Here I Am\\" not \\"All I Have\\". Sanderson has said himself that this is larger in scope than anything else he's written, and that's readily apparent in the story itself; so something as big as this world takes time to get rolling. However, I never felt like the story was lagging, or that things were moving too slowly. I enjoy reading about a new world and the people who inhabit it as much as I enjoy the action and mystery, so I drank in every detail that Sanderson put into this book.<br /><br />On the one hand, I wish I hadn't read this book because now I have to wait years for the next installment. On the other hand, I'm so glad I read this book because it opened up my mind creatively in a way that I've only experienced from a few novels, and I have read a TON of novels.

B003UESJH4|5.0|The King's Speech is one of those truly rare films that grabbed my attention and held it all the way through even though I knew how it would all end--this story is well known to many historians and some non-historians like myself know at least parts of the tale. The film succeeds because of the excellent acting by a five star cast; and the musical score is exceptional. The choreography and the cinematography impressed me as well. The plot moves along smoothly without being boring or excessively melodramatic.<br /><br />The film starts in 1925 and King George V of England (Michael Gambon) asks his second son, The Duke of York (Colin Firth), to make a closing speech at The Empire Exhibition in Wembley, London. The Duke of York dreads public speaking because he suffers from an embarrassing problem with stuttering. The speech does not go well; together he and his wife (Helena Bonham Carter, who would become Queen Elizabeth), try just about every doctor, specialist and speech therapist to no avail. After some time, however, the future Queen Elizabeth discovers an Australian speech therapist named Lionel Logue (Geoffrey Rush); and she convinces her husband to try Lionel's unconventional treatments which combine speech therapy with physical exercises, humor and some kindly psychotherapy.<br /><br />We also see things becoming more complicated with the passage of time. The Duke's father, King George V, grows old, becomes disoriented and dies; this makes his first son King Edward VIII (Guy Pearce). Yet King Edward VIII was romantically involved with a twice divorced woman named Wallis Simpson (Eve Best); and it isn't long before he abdicates the throne to marry her--leaving his brother, The Duke of York, still with a speech problem, in the uncomfortable position of having to ascend to the throne. Worse yet for the new King is that he becomes England's leader at a time when Europe was headed toward war with Germany and almost certainly other countries as well.<br /><br />I could tell you much more; I've already left out some of the plot and I won't write about the rest so I don't ruin it for you! Suffice it to say that the story plays out with elegance and poignancy and much credit is due to the actors, especially Colin Firth and Geoffrey Rush. In addition, look for solid performances by Roger Parrott as Neville Chamberlain; Timothy Spall as Winston Churchill; Jennifer Ehle as Myrtle Logue and Derek Jacobi as Archbishop Cosmo Lang.<br /><br />The DVD has an optional running commentary with director Tom Hooper; a featurette entitled The King's Speech: An Inspirational Story of an Unlikely Friendship; a Q&A session with the director and the cast; another featurette about the real Lionel Logue--and more!<br /><br />The King's Speech is a brilliant and most sensitive portrayal of the man who hesitantly became King George VI of England as he conquered his speech disability with the help of a kindly, unconventional therapist. The friendship these two men gradually develop is touching as well. The other characters in this film are faultlessly showcased, too. I recommend this film for anyone who likes inspiring dramas based very much on real life events; and fans of the actors in this film would do well to get this for their collections.I've already left out some of the plot and I won't write about the rest so I don't ruin it for you! We are bored with our lives, and so we decide to watch this movie, which, if we are VERY bored, we will switch off after fifteen minutes.<br /><br />Why is this movie so boring? Well, for one thing, it was directed and written by Nora Ephron, who simply cannot write anything interesting. For example, it would have been fascinating to follow Julia Child a little more closely as a real person. Instead we got Meryl Streep's relentlessly fruity Julia Child impersonation (peppered with some vague references to the McCarthy era, which Ephron thought would spice things up, but didn't). The reason Julie Powell was so boring is that she is, well, boring.  What could be less interesting that the quintessential chick flick heroine? She's awkward, incompetent (hey, I could poach an egg when I was nine), has dull, catty friends, and has an inferiority complex so thick you could make fillets out of it.<br /><br />The real question is: why do people like this boring stuff? Well, apparently we do so little with our lives that we can completely identify with a person who writes an inconsequential blog about a trivial topic (food, food, more food, and then feeling fat). Women were tossed out of their jobs and turned into \\"home-makers.\\" Well, being stuck at home after having run a factory is boring.  Julia Child attempted to make the oldest profession (the real oldest profession) more tolerable by introducing a challenge: How time-consuming can I make cooking dinner? It was Julia's small, but significant contribution to the castration of post WWII women.<br /><br />We have a similar problem now. 0443069522|4.0|This is an excellent book for an anatomy student who does better from reading a text book - as opposed to just looking at the pictures in Netter's or Thieme.  It is a real textbook that breaks things down into their simplest components - things like blood supply to the abdomen are broken down simply by developmental regions.  It is really helpful for getting big concepts and a basic orientation to the incredible onslaught of vocabulary and spatial memorization that is med school gross anatomy.  However, it is a little light on the details.  Some things are glossed over and not mentioned at all, most likely for the sake of simplicity.  For an undergrad anatomy class, it is perfect.  For a med school gross anatomy course, you really need a solid atlas to go a long with it to ensure you don't miss those pesky little details that anatomists love to toss into an exam.

1449382673|4.0|There are two books devoted to Python you can find within Head First series. One of them is related indirectly - Head First Programming, second one is explicitly focused on Python - Head First Python. If you take a look at the content description you will find that some parts of material overlaps. If you ask yourself whether these are two different titles, answer is - yes, these are two different books. Even thou both are devoted to Python, you will find them different. Head First Programming focuses on telling reader what programming is, while Head First Python goes directly into Python related topics. Another benefit of this book is that it touches various aspects of Python usage - application programming, Web development, Mobile development.<br /><br />What I like in the book is a way things are explained. Head First approach is one of favorite, so I like the book automatically - in a way. On the other hand, I think it could be better bundled with Head First Programming. Both books cover the same topics in few places which makes it questionable to buy both of them. Putting it straight. If you want to focus on Python itself, and you know what programming is all about - Head First Python is a good choice. If you don't know any programming language or you want to learn programming through the Python, definitely buy Head First Programming instead of this one.

B000005HNQ|5.0|Nighttime Birds, the fourth full-length recording from The Gathering and second with vocalist Anneke van Giersbergen, is essentially a further exploration of the style and sound of 1995's Mandylion, sharing with that work the same basic songwriting principles and agenda, and even a very similar studio production. As Mandylion was an excellent effort announcing the formal arrival of The Gathering as a pioneering band in female-fronted atmospheric metal, Nighttime Birds, as a stylistically faithful follow-up, is another fine album which can be summed up as a brighter, slightly more extroverted but less mysterious version of Mandylion.     \\"I am the snow falling down on you   I tear up your face with my frost   And make you run to somewhere warm\\"    Having discovered the vocal gem that is Anneke after two failed attempts in the lead vocal department, The Gathering cannot exactly be faulted for spotlighting her gorgeously expressive singing. While a good amount of these songs contain extended instrumental passages, Anneke's effervescent voice remains the focal point of The Gathering's presentation, and as fantastic as these musicians are, they play to the band's newfound communicative strength in these diverse alterations of verse/chorus form, in which Giersbergen's emotively dynamic and invigorating voice sings the defining melody with such life-affirming conviction and genuine feeling that even when reflective and melancholy there is an enlivening element coursing through the music that is incredibly inspiring.    The music is abundant with skyline-reflecting lead guitar melodies and ethereal synth washes that, particularly when converging in a moment of pure heavenly ascendance, can be quite spell-binding and enveloping. There is very well established counterbalance between sweeping atmospheric passages of floating beauty and powerful, riff-driven movements, a feature that works to great effect in opener \\"On Most Surfaces\\" and \\"Confusion\\", but perhaps most powerfullyexemplified in \\"New Moon, Different Day\\", which begins with first-rays-of-sunlight keyboards and reflective guitar chords, and concludes with a surprisingly crushing riff and slamming drums, a resolving climax rising in intensity like a newly awakened joyous perspective on existence emerging from a cloud of fears.    \\"The clouds make a circle   on the soil that I'm on   and I trust what surrounds me   I want to cross     I break the storm through the cloud\\"    What really makes this music fascinating and thoroughly engaging is its simply expressed, positive affection for small pleasures and observations often taken for granted in the thrust and swell of everyday living. It considers the world and all its phenomena, from its changing seasons, diverse landscapes, and activity of its wildlife, to the hopes and dreams of its human inhabitants, and embraces all of it in a most cheerful melancholy; yes, much of it is about feeling good despite reasons for tremendous sadness and dark despair, but it speaks to a childlike wonder and innocence in us that is regrettably too easily lost to the practical concerns of maturity, and does so with unmistakable sincerity and directly expressive beauty.

1416583432|4.0|The book is informative and entertaining, as far as it goes, but offers little in the way of practical advice. It does offer insights that will promote greater human understanding of dogs and concludes with a strong chapter that suggests ways humans can relate better to the dogs in their households. The book also does an outstanding job of describing dogs' sensory experience of the world, devoting nearly 100 pages to the subject.<br />But in its claim to present the canine perspective, the book falls short. Horowitz does decode some situations according to a canine point of view -- her discussion of doggy raincoats and the way that their tight embrace might make dogs feel \\"subdued\\" rather than protected is an amusing example. But an overwhelming anthropomorphic bias comes through. For instance, after lengthy and well-done sections describing dogs' vision and how it differs from humans' and explaining that smell is dogs' primary source of information, Horowitz attributes her dog's hesitance to enter the elevator to age-related deterioration of her vision or difficulty adjusting to low light after being outside. These are both reasons a human might hesitate. But the crevice between the floor and the elevator harbors many strange smells; this is an alternative reason, more in tune with what matters to dogs, that dogs might hesitate to enter elevators. Another (though unlikely in the case of Horowitz's apartment-dwelling dog) is an unpleasant memory of the moving floor.<br />Horowitz also stays loyal to her scientist roots in her reliance on research studies -- even poorly designed and executed studies -- to draw conclusions, even when real-life experience points to different conclusions. A study of dogs' reactions to \\"emergency\\" situations is instructive. In this study, humans set up a highly contrived scenario, first having owners introduce their dogs to a \\"friendly stranger\\" and then having the owners feign an emergency -- a heart attack, for example. None of the dogs tested did what the humans wanted them to do (seek help from the \\"stranger\\"). Calling this a \\"clever\\" experiment, Horowitz draws the conclusion that dogs \\"simply do not naturally recognize or react to an emergency situation.\\" A more obvious conclusion, and one that gives more credit to the dogs' intelligence, is that the dogs could tell that the people were faking -- none of the scents and signals that indicate true alarm or physical dysfunction would have been present in the \\"actors.\\" Some dogs do, in fact, react to emergency situations, even those that they have not been specifically trained for.<br />Horowitz relies exclusively on some studies that dogs \\"failed,\\" such as the mirror test for self-awareness and a test of whether dogs felt \\"guilt\\" if they \\"stole\\" a treat, in arriving at her limited conclusions about doggy consciousness and self-awareness. She fails to acknowledge (or notice?) that the tests cited are anthropocentric in design -- that is, they test things that are relevant to people but not to dogs -- and were conducted in unfamiliar, contrived environments where the dogs' behavior would be far from natural. Other research showing strong evidence of dog self-awareness is not mentioned. Finally, and despite a section at the beginning of the book chiding scientists' tendency to see one animal as representative of a species, she makes many broad statements about dog behavior that are seemingly based on her observations of her one dog.<br />Overall, the book provides a good description of dogs' sensory perceptions of the world, but I think that its conclusions about dog behavior, consciousness, and self-awareness are questionable.d did what the humans wanted them to do (seek help from the \\"stranger\\"). Calling this a \\"clever\\" experiment, Horowitz draws the conclusion that dogs \\"simply do not naturally recognize or react to an emergency situation.\\" A more obvious conclusion, and one that gives more credit to the dogs' intelligence, is that the dogs could tell that the people were faking -- none of the scents and signals that indicate true alarm or physical dysfunction would have been present in the \\"actors.\\" Some dogs do, in fact, react to emergency situations, even those that they have not been specifically trained for.    Horowitz relies exclusively on some studies that dogs \\"failed,\\" such as the mirror test for self-awareness and a test of whether dogs felt \\"guilt\\" if they \\"stole\\" a treat, in arriving at her limited conclusions about doggy consciousness and self-awareness. She fails to acknowledge (or notice?) that the tests cited are anthropocentric in design -- that is, they test things that are relevant to people but not to dogs -- and were conducted in unfamiliar, contrived environments where the dogs' behavior would be far from natural. Other research showing strong evidence of dog self-awareness is not mentioned. Finally, and despite a section at the beginning of the book chiding scientists' tendency to see one animal as representative of a species, she makes many broad statements about dog behavior that are seemingly based on her observations of her one dog.   Overall, the book provides a good description of dogs' sensory perceptions of the world, but I think that its conclusions about dog behavior, consciousness, and self-awareness are questionable.

0375758992|5.0|Alexandra Fuller's book, DON'T LET'S GO TO THE DOGS TONIGHT, is filled with imagery and color about her childhood in Africa.  She has provided details of a nomadic life with somewhat unpleasant undertones in a high-spirited tone that reveals a child's innocence in all the bewildering circumstances she finds herself.  Her loving acceptance, if not approval, of an alcoholic mother and footloose father are gratifying in a world that appears to be more interested in tawdry looks at family life.    This debut book is remarkable in its honesty.  Fuller doesn't shy away from incidents that most biographers would find hard to detail.  She does so with a unique humor and tolerance that recalls the innocence of youth when we are too delighted at being alive to be upset at disagreeable events.  She had moments of disgust and terror that she seemed able to set aside in childish acceptance, and then recalled the same events as an adult having a less charitable view.  Her ability to travel that road, and bring the reader with her, is a wonderful achievement.    Some of the prose and dialogue seemed somewhat amateurish to me.  I particularly found the numerous \\"ha ha\\" and \\"hee hee\\" asides to be disconcerting and unnecessary.  When a writer creates a humorous passage, interjections to underscore that humor need not be added.  The reader shouldn't need to be coaxed.  That said, her descriptions of the country and people (and the conditions in which they find themselves) are gloriously detailed.  Fuller is also able to illuminate Africa's beauty with her insightful writing style.  The reader is given acute visual and sensory impressions that bring the country alive.    Africa is a captivating country, full of beauty, violence and earthy existence.  Fuller has brought all that to life in DON\\"T LET'S GO TO THE DOGS.  I consider her memoir a must read.

0812550706|1.0|Firstly, I should make it clear that I loved the original 1970s short story on which this novel was based. I also love many of the characters in this book, including Ender; after reading reviews highlighting the lack of depth and plausibility of these characters, I would probably agree that I identify with Ender due to bad experiences I had at school (which had nothing to do with intellectual superiority, and everything to do with my poor social skills!).     However... I find it hard to understand how people cannot notice the deep themes of child abuse that run through this novel. From the struggle within Ender (the abused) to avoid becoming Peter (his first abuser), to \\"Uncle Graf\\" isolating Ender by removing him first from the protection of his family and then from friendships with his peers.     Though Graff and Peter say that they love Ender as he sleeps in bed, their behaviour isn't loving; it's psychological and physical torture. There is a particularly disturbing chapter where Graff watches the boys in the shower as they fight naked to the death. This follows from the scene where Graff explains how he plans to convince Ender that no matter what, no adult will ever come to his rescue. Interestingly, Graff is later prosecuted for his treatment of these boys; he is fully acquitted and offered a promotion.    What I found most disturbing of all was the way in which very young children were presented as being capable of adult speech, thought and actions. In fact I seem to remember it being stated several times that the characters didn't really feel like children/weren't really children. From my limited knowledge of the psychology of child abusers, this is a belief held by a subgroup.     I have read other criticisms of this book that object to the hero committing genocide and calculated murder while remaining virtually blameless. What I also find difficult to accept is that in the same way that the situations Ender was placed in make his acts of extreme violencemorally acceptable, the acts of child abuse carried out by Ender's 'teachers' are similarly justified. Even Peter, who tortured his young siblings and small animals and then manipulated his way into power, is apparently redeemed at the end of the book with no sign that he felt guilt or regretted hurting others.    I can definitely see how someone (especially a hurt and isolated boy) could find strength and gain focus in their life from this book. Equally, I can see how they could find justification for hurting others - apparently this is a recommended text for military recruits. I certainly would not recommend this to any child and would only recommend it to an adult who's child/boyfriend had already read it; this is so that the adult could offer a balanced argument against some of Card's distasteful implicit viewpoints.    In summary, this book caught my attention and taught me a lot about the warped psychology of certain individuals. But I don't think that was what the author intended.

0553386794|4.0|The beginning of a modern fantasy epic that has the potential to be very, very good. I have to admit that halfway through I was having trouble understanding what all the hype was about. Martin takes his sweet time setting up his dominoes before starting off a chain reaction that unravels quickly with quite a few satisfying and saddening twists and turns by the end. While the first half of the book reads quite slowly (there's a massive cast of characters to introduce and geography to be laid out) the second half is quite gripping. Martin definitely deserves credit for the development of his plot and some, but not all of the characters. There are three separate, but finely intertwined stories that develop quite independent of each other throughout the course of the novel and one gets just the briefest glances of how they may someday come thundering together.<br /><br />What's good? Martin constructs an interesting world that is heavily back-storied. In that respect, I suppose you could compare him to Tolkein. Families have lineages listed in an appendix and races and religions have histories that are deep. You can tell that Martin took as much care constructing the setting of his world and the tangential elements as he did with the main plot and characters. There's just a hint of magic and fantasy throughout most of the story that leaves you yearning for more. This is a world that's moved beyond magic and dragons - a time beyond the Age of Heroes and is mostly a story about men, and a few very cunning and clever women, who play out their lives doing what men throughout history have always done: they fight over territory, women, pride and honor to the disaster of their fellow men. Not to worry though, Martin starts to deliver and we do see some traditional fantasy elements begin to rear their heads by the end, which entices us into the second book. The politicking and intrigue is layered and keeps you guessing throughout most of this work and a lot of the predictions I made for where the story was going failed to materialize, which was actually quite pleasant. It feels like he's doing something quite unexpected and different from the usual vein of the fantasy genre. The characters are wonderfully round too, much to young Sansa's (who believes in epic tales of good knights and evil villains; a character not unlike the typical fantasy reader) despair. Noble men like Eddard Stark are blinded by their preoccupation with duty and honor into impracticality while crude and barbaric individuals display the occasional and inexplicable kindness, perhaps spurred by a memory of their past or a desire to be something more human for once. You find yourself rooting for some characters to be vindicated and ridiculously pleased when things turn against those you despise. Favorite among these is Tyrion Lannister, the misshapen dwarf brother of some of the main antagonists in this volume. It reminds me a lot of what Moore did for comic book superheroes in the 1980s with Watchmen.<br /><br />What's bad? I think Martin is trying too hard to be a rogue among epic high fantasy writers. Yes, it's refreshing to get characters with a bit more gray in this genre rather than the stark blacks and whites and naivet&eacute; that are symptomatic of the poorer novels in this field, but it's easy to see how he can sometimes go overboard with the blood and rape and pillaging and the like. Maybe that's some people's cup of tea, but I could do without graphic depictions of rape several times throughout the course of a single volume of a multi-part epic. It's enough to introduce the idea that war is bad. Anyone with a shred of knowledge of history knows how terribly unpleasant it is to be among the losers in a war, especially if you're female. The casualness of these scenes is unpleasant and highly uncomfortable. Some people might argue that that was the intent of the author, and that's all well and good, but this series hardly strikes me as one that aims to break societal conventions and get us to rethink our culture and values. It's very nature is escapist entertainment and I don't like repeated instances of rape, especially of young girls, in my escapist entertainment and it made me uncomfortable enough to make me drop it a star in my rating. Other reviewers complained that the multiple sex scenes distracted from the narrative as a whole or were too explicit, but I don't necessarily agree with that. Yes, this is an R-rated book, but it's not smut fiction. As I mentioned earlier the beginning takes a long time to play out too. 0553573403|5.0|The moment I turned the last page of Game of Thrones by George R.R. Martin, I immediately checked to see if there was a copy available at my local library for the next book, Clash of Kings. There it was, all 1000 pages of it. I breathed a sigh of relief. You have before you a penitent, former skeptic, now convert. I get it. It may be 100 degrees outside, but my friends, Winter is Coming!!!<br /><br />Let me back up a little as to how my book club decided to read Game of Thrones for the month of June 2011 in the first place. You see we are fiery women who do not like being told what we ought to like or not like to read. We took offense, as many other women did, at New York Times' Ginia Bellafante's claim that HBO threw in a bunch of sex scenes in its adaptation of the book to lure women who would otherwise not touch Game of Thrones:<br /><br />\\"The true perversion, though, is the sense you get that all of this illicitness has been tossed in as a little something for the ladies, out of a justifiable fear, perhaps, that no woman alive would watch otherwise. While I do not doubt that there are women in the world who read books like Mr. Martin's, I can honestly say that I have never met a single woman who has stood up in indignation at her book club and refused to read the latest from Lorrie Moore unless everyone agreed to \\"The Hobbit\\" first. \\"Game of Thrones\\" is boy fiction patronizingly turned out to reach the population's other half.\\"<br /><br />Can I get a \\"Oh, no she didn't!\\" Bellafante has been raked repeatedly over the coals on this one so I won't go into the all sorts of wrong, wrong, and wrong goin' on in her abyss of ignorance. Let's just talk about the good stuff, shall we?<br /><br />First, although I do read fantasy on occasion, I have to be lured to it with the promise of lots and lots of sex scenes :) Unfortunately, there is a paucity of sex scenes in a book of this length and not that many scenes of knitting or shopping and, I have to caution you gals, no matchmaking balls whatsoever. However did it entertain this female reader?<br /><br />The writing, the world building, and the characters - all spellbinding. I read the first chapter and shivered with delight (and from the ominous catch phrase woven throughout the book \\"Winter is coming.\\" - cool in all the right ways during a hot summer)<br /><br />\\"Will rose. Ser Waymar Royce stood over him.<br /><br />His fine clothes were a tatter, his face a ruin. A shard from his sword transfixed the blind white pupil of his left eye.<br /><br />The right eye was open. The pupil burned blue. It saw.<br /><br />The broken sword fell from nerveless fingers. Will closed his eyes to pray. Long, elegant hands brushed his cheek, then tightened around his throat. They were gloved in the finest moleskin and sticky with blood, yet the touch was icy cold.\\"<br /><br />Game of Thrones' first surprise was although it has fantasy elements, this first of the series has more in common with Wolf Hall by Hilary Mantel than Lord of the Rings. The royal court is full of political intrigue and treachery by the power-hungry; I could not help but compare it to reading about the Tudors. As the title indicates, there are several kings, would-be kings, and usurpers vying for control. The Starks, a noble family far north of the Seven Kingdoms, have been ensnared in the dangerous web of the King's Court when Lord Eddard reluctantly becomes the King's Hand (think of him as Thomas Cromwell to Henry VIII). His unswerving loyalty to the King, a loveable man of large appetites, leads to tragedy as those in court double-deal, even triple-deal in their ruthless pursuit of power.<br /><br />Martin's brilliance to me lies in the characters he chose to tell the interweaving stories. There are many characters and many families in Game of Thrones (not to fear, though, lineage charts in the back of the book). Rather than focusing only on the heroic and larger than life characters that typically people fantasy books,we see this world through the outcasts' perspectives: Lord Eddard's bastard outcast son, Jon Snow; Lord Eddard's tomboy daughter, Arya; the King's disrespected brother-in-law, the dwarf Tyrion; and the child bride of the King of the Dothraki, Dani.<br /><br />And this is where Game of Thrones won me (and broke my heart a couple of times. I actually yelled, \\"NO! in anguish at a scene when a beloved character came to harm. Mr. Martin, if you kill off Bran, I will never forgive you. Ever.) The novel is set in a medieval-ish, feudal society where the men rule and women are mere helpmates and child-bearers. Or so I first thought. During the first few chapters, I marveled at the writing but groaned when I thought Martin might prove Bellafante right.<br /><br />Yes, it is a patriarchal society, but I will make the argument that it is the women - Lady Catelyn Stark, Cersei Lannister, and Dani - who drive the story. If it were not for Lady Stark and Queen Cersei, the Seven Kingdoms would not have been plunged into civil war. And Dani, oh Dani- how I dismissed you at first as a weak trophy wife beneath my interest. Well, folks, I'm here to tell you that hers is the most riveting of the storylines. From where she begins to where she ends is a magnificent journey that simply stunned me. Everything about Game of Thrones blew my expectations.<br /><br />\\"'You are a woman, my lady,' the Greatjon rumbled in his deep voice. 'Women do not understand these things.'<br /><br />\\"'You are the gentle sex,' said Lord Karstark, with the lines of grief fresh on his face. 'A man has need for vengeance.'<br /><br />\\"'Give me Cersei Lannister, Lord Karstark, and you would see how gentle a woman can be,' Catelyn replied.\\"<br /><br />Wanting desperately to know what happens to rebellious Jon Snow, sweet Bran, Dani, and plucky Arya is what has got me pledging my summer, fall, winter, and in all probability spring to the Song of Ice and Fire.<br /><br />Incidentally, Game of Thrones led to the first really vigorous discussion I've ever had with my dad over a book. To my knock-me-over-with-a-feather surprise, I found out that he is a die hard a fan of many years. The way I feel about Bran is the way my dad feels about the King (only my dad eventually forgave Mr. Martin for killing him off, after much bitterness).n, Jon Snow; Lord Eddard's tomboy daughter, Arya; the King's disrespected brother-in-law, the dwarf Tyrion; and the child bride of the King of the Dothraki, Dani. B0025KVLS8|5.0|This is the sound of Beatlemania.  This is the sound that changed the landscape of popular music. It is the sound that impelled thousands of young men to pick up guitars and learn to write their own songs.  This one.  Not \\"Rubber Soul,\\" nor \\"Magical Mystery Tour\\". Absent is the mannerism of \\"Revolver\\" and \\"Sergeant Pepper\\" (not that those two are not outstanding albums, but they lack the spontaneity evident on every track on this singular work).  It is not an accident that it is very much John Lennon's album.  Only three tracks are Paul's.  John wrote \\"A Hard Day's Night\\" in one night.  McCartney, if asked, would probably say he helped John on each one - but that is Paul's ego speaking which is harder than Everest to climb and just as foggy.  Not one croaker in the bunch.  Make no kistake, in 1964 this was as hard as rock had ever been.  Listen to their competition: Dean Martin, Ferrante and Teicher, the derivitiveness of early Beach Boys (\\"Fun, Fun, Fun,\\" \\"Surfin USA\\"), Fabian, a mellowed out and gutless Elvis, and the once great Louis Armstrong.  Fifty percent of all popular music sold in the early sixties was instrumental. This album and the film to which its acts as soundtrack changed that (even the bastardized US version featured abysmal instrumentals). Their first two albums were fresh but it was in this album that their songwriting skills came into their own and they soared with confidence and energy the like of which has never hit the airwaves before (and perhaps since).  If the group had released nothing after this, they would still rank in the Pantheon for this effort alone.  To these ears, it ranks as one of their two greatest albums.

0345444051|5.0|Sir Arthur's early effort in speculative science fiction is an astounding piece of story-telling. Although Clarke himself has expressed that this novel is not his personal best, it at least must be one of his most important. Childhood's End refers to the end of humanity as we know it - no, this is not a disaster or dystopian extrapolation, it is humanity's first step into an 'adulthood' of true understanding, extra-sensorary evolution and an era of meaning and purpose for the race.<br /><br />Like many of Clarke's works, the scope of this novel is phenomenal. From the appearance of starships hovering over every major city on the planet (a notion borrowed liberally by countless films), we know that humanity will never be the same again. How would humankind react to irrefuteable proof of extra-terrestrial life? Or benign, superior alien life, to be more precise? Shock, anger, acceptance, indifference are but a sample of humankind's somewhat fickle reactions to the Overlords' presence. Their purpose is mysterious, but their impact is staggering: the atrocities of the previous eras come to a close overnight, for what use is it to squabble among ourselves when all secrets are observed, known and occasionally controlled by the Overlords?<br /><br />The novel shows us more about human nature than any other intelligence in the novel (and let me tell you, there is more than one other intelligence described). In this sense, there is a wealth of social psychology to consider, but Clarke's excellent pacing ensures we're never bogged down too deeply in any one element of this admitedly grandiose tale. Hard science fiction fans are catered to as well - the intricacies of Eisteinian time dilation is used as a unique plot mechanism. This shows that reality really does provide circumstances 'queerer than we can imagine' while impressing to this reader of Clarke's daring and grasp of fiction and non-fiction alike.
1594744769|5.0|This book called to me and insisted I buy it! I swear that little girl on the cover got into my head. It took me 2-3 days to read it, during which I took it with me on the bus, and continued reading it while walking up the sidewalk to my destinations. (I rarely read while walking, but this book had me in its clutches, and I was in quiet neighborhoods, not busy downtown areas).  The plot was great.  A boy seeking answers to his Grandfather's past and sudden death visits the ruins of the place where his Grandfather grew up, and suddenly becomes more involved with the past than he'd ever dreamed of.  The Peculiar Children in the photographs spring to life, and provide many answers.    The photos themselves are an integral part of the story, each picture introduced at the appropriate point, so there is never any doubt how the pictures fit into the overall plot.  The pictures themselves are as peculiar as their subjects, using old time photographic techniques to create the strangeness of the images, rather than relying on modern digital technology.      I have been waiting for another truly enchanting book since I finished reading the Harry Potter series, and finally found it here.  Miss Peregrine's Home for Peculiar Children is a truly enchanting book that casts a spell on the reader that lasts long after the last page is read.  I am fervently hoping for sequels!

0066620996|4.0|This is a book that will probably give readers a taste of what it's like to run a successful business, or at least what it's like to watch a successful business run. In this vein, it provides a handful of good concepts that will give entrepreneurs and executives pause before they sign on to the latest cliches of management. The concept of the fly-wheel (keep churning and it will get easier and more successful with each turn), the three aspects of a successful business idea, and the concept that good workers make the most significant difference in a company's success were the most valuable take-aways.<br /><br />I do have my criticisms. First, the methodology--the study was conducted very scientifically, but only on a portion of the companies. They used performance to identify their cases, then went backward to see what went right. Then they studied similar companies that didn't have the same success and went backward to see what went wrong. They did not investigate companies that did what the successful companies did and still failed, or companies that did not do what the case companies did and succeeded. Doing this would have made it a more comprehensive study.<br /><br />Second, the depth--the strength of this book is making powerful concepts accessible and memorable. \\"Get the right people on the bus,\\" \\"Hedgehog concept,\\" \\"Wash your cottage cheese,\\" etc. are all useful images that evoke what could be powerful management tools. The problem is that the author repeats these concepts ad nauseam and at the expense of real depth in the logic. As a result, the reader will remember the concepts, but might not be able to apply them in unique circumstances.<br /><br />It could be that I am looking for something that this book is not meant to provide, in which case I would love if someone could provide leads on books that cover the theory behind these concepts: Why is \\"Who, then What\\" successful? Why does modesty in leadership work over cockiness? How does bureaucracy form, how can it be diminished, and how does it affect workers and productivity?<br /><br />Third, the logic--It is possible that much of the logic is out there somewhere, and the lack of depth makes the arguments seem incongruent, but I could not agree with some of the book's central themes. For instance, \\"Who, then What\\" simply doesn't make sense.
